class="infobox-below" style="border-top:1px #aaa solid"><div style="width:100%"><div style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;" class="noprint"><b>Preceded&#160;by</b><br /><a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1922%E2%80%9369)" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Republican Army (1922\xe2\x80\x9369)">Irish Republican Army</a> (IRA)</div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Irish Republican Army</b> (<b>IRA</b>; <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <i lang="ga"><a href="/wiki/%C3%93glaigh_na_h%C3%89ireann" title="\xc3\x93glaigh na h\xc3\x89ireann">\xc3\x93glaigh na h\xc3\x89ireann</a></i>), also known as the <b>Provisional Irish Republican Army</b>, and informally as the <b>Provos</b>, was an <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">Irish republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> organisation that sought to end British rule in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, facilitate <a href="/wiki/United_Ireland" title="United Ireland">Irish reunification</a> and bring about an independent, socialist <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a> encompassing all of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>. It was the most active republican paramilitary group during <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">the Troubles</a>. It saw itself as the army of the all-island <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republic" title="Irish Republic">Irish Republic</a> and as the sole legitimate successor to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1919%E2%80%931922)" title="Irish Republican Army (1919\xe2\x80\x931922)">original IRA</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a>. It was designated a <a href="/wiki/Terrorist_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Terrorist organisation">terrorist organisation</a> in the United Kingdom and an illegal organisation in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>, both of whose authority it rejected.\n</p><p>The Provisional IRA emerged in December 1969, due to a split within <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1922%E2%80%931969)" title="Irish Republican Army (1922\xe2\x80\x931969)">the previous incarnation of the IRA</a> and the broader <a href="/wiki/Republican_movement_(Ireland)" title="Republican movement (Ireland)">Irish republican movement</a>. It was initially the minority faction in the split compared to the <a href="/wiki/Official_IRA" class="mw-redirect" title="Official IRA">Official IRA</a>, but became the dominant faction by 1972. The Troubles <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles#Late_1960s" title="The Troubles">had begun</a> shortly before when a largely Catholic, nonviolent <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_civil-rights_movement_(1960s)" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Ireland civil-rights movement (1960s)">civil rights campaign</a> was met with violence from both <a href="/wiki/Ulster_loyalism" title="Ulster loyalism">Ulster loyalists</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a> (RUC), culminating in the <a href="/wiki/1969_Northern_Ireland_riots" title="1969 Northern Ireland riots">August 1969 riots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Banner" title="Operation Banner">deployment of British soldiers</a>. The IRA initially focused on defence of Catholic areas, but it began <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army_campaign_1969%E2%80%9397" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army campaign 1969\xe2\x80\x9397">an offensive campaign in 1970</a> that was aided by weapons supplied by <a href="/wiki/Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish American">Irish American</a> sympathisers and Libyan leader <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a>. It used <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla</a> tactics against the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and RUC in both rural and urban areas, and carried out a bombing campaign in Northern Ireland and England against military, political, and economic targets, and British military targets in Europe.\n</p><p>The Provisional IRA declared a final ceasefire in July 1997, after which its political wing <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> was admitted into multi-party peace talks on the future of Northern Ireland. These resulted in the 1998 <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>, and in 2005 the IRA formally ended its armed campaign and <a href="/wiki/Decommissioning_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Decommissioning in Northern Ireland">decommissioned its weapons</a> under the supervision of the <a href="/wiki/Independent_International_Commission_on_Decommissioning" title="Independent International Commission on Decommissioning">Independent International Commission on Decommissioning</a>. Several <a href="/wiki/Splinter_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Splinter group">splinter groups</a> have been formed as a result of splits within the IRA, including the <a href="/wiki/Continuity_IRA" class="mw-redirect" title="Continuity IRA">Continuity IRA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Real_IRA" class="mw-redirect" title="Real IRA">Real IRA</a>, both of which are still active in the <a href="/wiki/Dissident_Irish_republican_campaign" title="Dissident Irish republican campaign">dissident Irish republican campaign</a>. The IRA\'s armed campaign, primarily in Northern Ireland but also in England and mainland Europe, killed over 1,700 people, including roughly 1,000 members of the British security forces, and 500\xe2\x80\x93644 civilians. In addition 275\xe2\x80\x93300 members of the IRA were killed during the conflict.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Initial phase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#The_&quot;Long_War&quot;"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">The "Long War"</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Peace_process"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Peace process</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#End_of_the_armed_campaign"><span class="tocnumber">1.6</span> <span class="toctext">End of the armed campaign</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Weaponry_and_operations"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Weaponry and operations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Casualties"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Casualties</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Structure"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Structure</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Political_ideology"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Political ideology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Categorisation"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Categorisation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Strength_and_support"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Strength and support</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Numerical_strength"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Numerical strength</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Support_from_other_countries_and_organisations"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Support from other countries and organisations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Other_activities"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Other activities</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Sectarian_attacks"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Sectarian attacks</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Financing"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Financing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Vigilantism"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Vigilantism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Informers"><span class="tocnumber">8.4</span> <span class="toctext">Informers</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Splinter_groups"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Splinter groups</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Notes_and_references"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes and references</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army_campaign_1969%E2%80%9397" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army campaign 1969\xe2\x80\x9397">Provisional Irish Republican Army campaign 1969\xe2\x80\x9397</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Northern_Ireland" title="History of Northern Ireland">History of Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/220px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="334" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/330px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/440px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png 2x" data-file-width="954" data-file-height="1450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Proclamation_of_the_Irish_Republic" title="Proclamation of the Irish Republic">Proclamation of the Irish Republic</a>, issued during the 1916 <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> against British rule in Ireland</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1919%E2%80%931922)" title="Irish Republican Army (1919\xe2\x80\x931922)">original IRA</a> was formed in 1913 as the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Volunteers" title="Irish Volunteers">Irish Volunteers</a>, at a time when all of Ireland was part of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-taylororigins_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylororigins-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The Volunteers took part in the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> against <a href="/wiki/British_rule_in_Ireland" title="British rule in Ireland">British rule</a> in 1916, and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">War of Independence</a> that followed the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Irish Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> by the revolutionary parliament <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1il_%C3%89ireann_(Irish_Republic)" title="D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann (Irish Republic)">D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann</a> in 1919, during which they came to be known as the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-taylororigins_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylororigins-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">Ireland was partitioned</a> into <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ireland_(1921%E2%80%9322)" title="Southern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x9322)">Southern Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1920" title="Government of Ireland Act 1920">Government of Ireland Act 1920</a>, and following the implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a> in 1922 Southern Ireland, renamed the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, became a self-governing <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominion</a> while Northern Ireland chose to remain under <a href="/wiki/Home_rule" title="Home rule">home rule</a> as part of the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The Treaty caused a split in the IRA, the pro-Treaty IRA were absorbed into the <a href="/wiki/National_Army_(Ireland)" title="National Army (Ireland)">National Army</a>, which defeated the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1922%E2%80%931969)" title="Irish Republican Army (1922\xe2\x80\x931969)">anti-Treaty IRA</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Civil_War" title="Irish Civil War">Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-white1921_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white1921-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently, while denying the legitimacy of the Free State, the surviving elements of the anti-Treaty IRA focused on overthrowing the Northern Ireland state and the achievement of a <a href="/wiki/United_Ireland" title="United Ireland">united Ireland</a>, carrying out a <a href="/wiki/S-Plan" title="S-Plan">bombing campaign in England in 1939 and 1940</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Northern_campaign_(Irish_Republican_Army)" title="Northern campaign (Irish Republican Army)">campaign in Northern Ireland in the 1940s</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Border_campaign_(Irish_Republican_Army)" title="Border campaign (Irish Republican Army)">Border campaign of 1956\xe2\x80\x931962</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Following the failure of the Border campaign, internal debate took place regarding the future of the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Chief-of-staff <a href="/wiki/Cathal_Goulding" title="Cathal Goulding">Cathal Goulding</a> wanted the IRA to adopt a <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> agenda and become involved in politics, while traditional republicans such as <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_Mac_St%C3%ADof%C3%A1in" title="Se\xc3\xa1n Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in">Se\xc3\xa1n Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in</a> wanted to increase recruitment and rebuild the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following partition Northern Ireland became a <a href="/wiki/Dominant-party_system" title="Dominant-party system">de facto one-party state</a> governed by the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist Party</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Parliament of Northern Ireland</a>, in which Catholics viewed themselves as <a href="/wiki/Second-class_citizen" title="Second-class citizen">second-class citizens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestants</a> were given preference in jobs and housing, and <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Local government in Northern Ireland">local government constituencies</a> were <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandered" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerrymandered">gerrymandered</a> in places such as <a href="/wiki/Derry" title="Derry">Derry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tayloruvf_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tayloruvf-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Policing was carried out by the armed <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a> (RUC) and the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Special_Constabulary" title="Ulster Special Constabulary">B-Specials</a>, both of which were almost exclusively Protestant.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In the mid-1960s tension between the Catholic and Protestant communities was increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-tayloruvf_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tayloruvf-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In 1966 Ireland celebrated the 50th anniversary of the Easter Rising, prompting fears of a renewed IRA campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Feeling under threat, Protestants formed the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteer_Force" title="Ulster Volunteer Force">Ulster Volunteer Force</a> (UVF), a <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> group which killed three people in May 1966, two of them Catholic men.<sup id="cite_ref-tayloruvf_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tayloruvf-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In January 1967 the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Civil_Rights_Association" title="Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association">Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association</a> (NICRA) was formed by a diverse group of people, including IRA members and liberal <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">unionists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-whitecr_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitecr-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Civil rights marches by NICRA and a similar organisation, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democracy_(Ireland)" title="People&#39;s Democracy (Ireland)">People\'s Democracy</a>, protesting against discrimination were met by <a href="/wiki/Counter-protest" title="Counter-protest">counter-protests</a> and violent clashes with <a href="/wiki/Ulster_loyalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster loyalist">loyalists</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Protestant_Volunteers" title="Ulster Protestant Volunteers">Ulster Protestant Volunteers</a>, a paramilitary group led by <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Marches marking the Ulster Protestant celebration <a href="/wiki/The_Twelfth" title="The Twelfth">The Twelfth</a> in July 1969 led to riots and violent clashes in <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>, Derry and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The following month a three-day riot began in the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Bogside" title="Bogside">Bogside</a> area of Derry, following a march by the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Apprentice_Boys_of_Derry" title="Apprentice Boys of Derry">Apprentice Boys of Derry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorbogside_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorbogside-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bogside" title="Battle of the Bogside">Battle of the Bogside</a> caused Catholics in Belfast to riot in <a href="/wiki/Solidarity" title="Solidarity">solidarity</a> with the Bogsiders and to try to prevent RUC reinforcements being sent to Derry, sparking retaliation by Protestant mobs.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Arson_attack" class="mw-redirect" title="Arson attack">arson attacks</a>, damage to property and intimidation forced 1,505 Catholic families and 315 Protestant families to leave their homes in Belfast in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_riots_of_August_1969" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Ireland riots of August 1969">Northern Ireland riots of August 1969</a><sup id="cite_ref-mb117_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mb117-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The riots resulted in 275 buildings being destroyed or requiring major repairs, 83.5% of them occupied by Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-mb117_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mb117-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> A number of people were killed on both sides, some by the police, and the British Army were <a href="/wiki/Operation_Banner" title="Operation Banner">deployed to Northern Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor1969_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1969-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The IRA had been poorly armed and failed to properly defend Catholic areas from Protestant attacks,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> which had been considered one of its roles since the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Veteran republicans were critical of Goulding and the IRA\'s Dublin leadership which, for political reasons, had refused to prepare for aggressive action in advance of the violence.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> On 24 August a group including <a href="/wiki/Joe_Cahill" title="Joe Cahill">Joe Cahill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seamus_Twomey" title="Seamus Twomey">Seamus Twomey</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1ith%C3%AD_%C3%93_Conaill" title="D\xc3\xa1ith\xc3\xad \xc3\x93 Conaill">D\xc3\xa1ith\xc3\xad \xc3\x93 Conaill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billy_McKee" title="Billy McKee">Billy McKee</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Steele_(republican)" title="Jimmy Steele (republican)">Jimmy Steele</a> came together in Belfast and decided to remove the pro-Goulding Belfast leadership of <a href="/wiki/Billy_McMillen" title="Billy McMillen">Billy McMillen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Sullivan_(Irish_republican)" title="Jim Sullivan (Irish republican)">Jim Sullivan</a> and return to traditional militant republicanism.<sup id="cite_ref-mb125_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mb125-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> On 22 September Twomey, McKee, and Steele were among sixteen armed IRA men who confronted the Belfast leadership over the failure to adequately defend Catholic areas.<sup id="cite_ref-mb125_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mb125-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> A compromise was agreed where McMillen stayed in command, but he was not to have any communication with the IRA\'s Dublin based leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-mb125_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mb125-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1969_split">1969 split</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1969 split">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh_2004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh_2004.jpg/220px-Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh_2004.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh_2004.jpg/330px-Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh_2004.jpg/440px-Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh_2004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="542" data-file-height="616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh_2004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh" title="Ruair\xc3\xad \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh">Ruair\xc3\xad \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh</a>, who was twice <a href="/wiki/List_of_Irish_Republican_Army_chiefs_of_staff" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Irish Republican Army chiefs of staff">chief-of-staff</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1922%E2%80%931969)" title="Irish Republican Army (1922\xe2\x80\x931969)">pre-1969 IRA</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Border_campaign_(Irish_Republican_Army)" title="Border campaign (Irish Republican Army)">Border campaign of 1956\xe2\x80\x931962</a>, was a member of the first <a href="/wiki/IRA_Army_Council" title="IRA Army Council">Army Council</a> of the Provisional IRA in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-mallie137_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mallie137-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The IRA split into "Provisional" and <a href="/wiki/Official_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Official Irish Republican Army">"Official"</a> factions in December 1969,<sup id="cite_ref-taylorsplit_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorsplit-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> after an IRA convention was held in <a href="/wiki/Boyle,_County_Roscommon" title="Boyle, County Roscommon">Boyle, County Roscommon</a>, Republic of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-white1969gac_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white1969gac-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hanley145_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hanley145-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The two main issues at the convention were a <a href="/wiki/Resolution_(debate)" class="mw-redirect" title="Resolution (debate)">resolution</a> to enter into a "National Liberation Front" with radical left-wing groups, and a resolution to end <a href="/wiki/Abstentionism" title="Abstentionism">abstentionism</a>, which would allow participation in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">British</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1il_%C3%89ireann" title="D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann">Irish</a>, and Northern Ireland parliaments.<sup id="cite_ref-white1969gac_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white1969gac-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Traditional republicans refused to vote on the "National Liberation Front" and it was passed by twenty-nine votes to seven.<sup id="cite_ref-white1969gac_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white1969gac-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-horgantaylor_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horgantaylor-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The traditionalists argued strongly against the ending of abstentionism, and the <a href="/wiki/Minutes" title="Minutes">official minutes</a> report the resolution passed by twenty-seven votes to twelve.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;n 3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-white1969gac_53-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white1969gac-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-horgantaylor_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horgantaylor-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Following the convention the traditionalists canvassed support throughout Ireland, with IRA director of intelligence Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in meeting the disaffected members of the IRA in Belfast.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after, the traditionalists held a convention which elected a <a href="/wiki/IRA_Army_Council" title="IRA Army Council">"Provisional" Army Council</a>, composed of Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in, <a href="/wiki/Ruair%C3%AD_%C3%93_Br%C3%A1daigh" title="Ruair\xc3\xad \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh">Ruair\xc3\xad \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paddy_Mulcahy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Paddy Mulcahy (page does not exist)">Paddy Mulcahy</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sean_Tracey_(Irish_republican)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sean Tracey (Irish republican) (page does not exist)">Sean Tracey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leo_Martin_(Irish_republican)" title="Leo Martin (Irish republican)">Leo Martin</a>, \xc3\x93 Conaill, and Cahill.<sup id="cite_ref-mallie137_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mallie137-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The term provisional was chosen to mirror the 1916 <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_the_Irish_Republic" title="Provisional Government of the Irish Republic">Provisional Government of the Irish Republic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-white1969gac_53-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white1969gac-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and also to designate it as temporary pending <a href="/wiki/Ratification" title="Ratification">ratification</a> by a further IRA convention.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mallie137_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mallie137-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Nine out of thirteen IRA units in Belfast sided with the "Provisional" Army Council in December 1969, roughly 120 activists and 500 supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The Provisional IRA issued their first public statement on 28 December 1969,<sup id="cite_ref-allegiance_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allegiance-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> stating:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>We declare our allegiance to the 32 county Irish republic, proclaimed at Easter 1916, established by the first D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann in 1919, overthrown by force of arms in 1922 and suppressed to this day by the existing British-imposed six-county and twenty-six-county partition states<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... We call on the Irish people at home and in exile for increased support towards defending our people in the North and the eventual achievement of the full political, social, economic and cultural freedom of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;n 5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bowyerbell1969_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowyerbell1969-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The Irish republican political party <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> split along the same lines on 11 January 1970 in Dublin, when a third of the delegates walked out of the party\'s highest deliberative body, the <a href="/wiki/Ard_fheis" class="mw-redirect" title="Ard fheis">ard fheis</a>, in protest at the party leadership\'s attempt to force through the ending of abstentionism, despite its failure to achieve a two-thirds majority vote of delegates required to change the policy.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;n 6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylorsplit_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorsplit-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The delegates that walked out reconvened at another venue where Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in, \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh and Mulcahy from the "Provisional" Army Council were elected to the Caretaker Executive of "Provisional" Sinn F\xc3\xa9in.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;n 7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Despite the declared support of that faction of Sinn F\xc3\xa9in, the early Provisional IRA avoided political activity, instead relying on <a href="/wiki/Physical_force_republicanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical force republicanism">physical force republicanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> \xc2\xa3100,000 was donated by the <a href="/wiki/Fianna_F%C3%A1il" title="Fianna F\xc3\xa1il">Fianna F\xc3\xa1il</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Irish_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish government">Irish government</a> in 1969 to "defence committees" in Catholic areas, some of which ended up in the hands of the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-armstrial_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armstrial-73">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> This resulted in the 1970 <a href="/wiki/Arms_Crisis" title="Arms Crisis">Arms Crisis</a> where criminal charges were pursued against two former government ministers and others including <a href="/wiki/John_Kelly_(Sinn_F%C3%A9in_politician)" title="John Kelly (Sinn F\xc3\xa9in politician)">John Kelly</a>, an IRA <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_(Irish_republican)" title="Volunteer (Irish republican)">volunteer</a> from Belfast.<sup id="cite_ref-armstrial_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armstrial-73">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The Provisional IRA maintained the principles of the pre-1969 IRA, considering both British rule in Northern Ireland and the government of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> to be illegitimate, and the Army Council to be the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_government" title="Provisional government">provisional government</a> of the all-island <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republic" title="Irish Republic">Irish Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> This belief was based on a series of <a href="/wiki/Irish_republican_legitimatism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish republican legitimatism">perceived political inheritances</a> which constructed a legal continuity from the <a href="/wiki/Second_D%C3%A1il" title="Second D\xc3\xa1il">Second D\xc3\xa1il</a> of 1921\xe2\x80\x931922.<sup id="cite_ref-obrien104_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-obrien104-77">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The IRA recruited many young nationalists from Northern Ireland who had not been involved in the IRA before, but had been radicalised by the violence that broke out in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloney80_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloney80-79">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> These people became known as "sixty niners", having joined after 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;n 8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloney80_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloney80-79">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The IRA adopted the <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(mythology)" title="Phoenix (mythology)">phoenix</a> as the symbol of the Irish republican rebirth in 1969, one of its slogans was "out of the ashes rose the Provisionals", representing the IRA\'s resurrection from the ashes of burnt-out Catholic areas of Belfast.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_phase">Initial phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Initial phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_McGuinness_MLA_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Martin_McGuinness_MLA_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Martin_McGuinness_MLA_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Martin_McGuinness_MLA_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Martin_McGuinness_MLA_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Martin_McGuinness_MLA_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Martin_McGuinness_MLA_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1196" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_McGuinness_MLA_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martin_McGuinness" title="Martin McGuinness">Martin McGuinness</a> was part of an IRA delegation which took part in peace talks with British politician <a href="/wiki/William_Whitelaw" class="mw-redirect" title="William Whitelaw">William Whitelaw</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Secretary of State for Northern Ireland">Secretary of State for Northern Ireland</a>, in July 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor1972_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1972-85">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In January 1970 the Army Council decided to adopt a three-stage strategy; defence of nationalist areas, followed by a combination of defence and retaliation, and finally launching a guerrilla campaign against the British Army.<sup id="cite_ref-english125_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-english125-86">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The Official IRA was opposed to such a campaign because they felt it would lead to sectarian conflict, which would defeat their strategy of uniting the workers from both sides of the sectarian divide.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The Provisional IRA\'s strategy was to use force to cause the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Northern_Ireland_(1921%E2%80%931972)" title="Government of Northern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x931972)">Northern Ireland government</a> and to inflict such heavy casualties on the British Army that the British government would be forced by public opinion to withdraw from Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-smith9799_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith9799-88">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in decided they would "escalate, escalate and escalate", in what the British Army would later describe as a "classic <a href="/wiki/Insurgency" title="Insurgency">insurgency</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In October 1970 the IRA began a bombing campaign against economic targets; by the end of the year there had been 153 explosions.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The following year it was responsible for the vast majority of the 1,000 explosions that occurred in Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The strategic aim behind the bombings was to target businesses and commercial premises to deter investment and force the British government to pay compensation, increasing the financial cost of keeping Northern Ireland within the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;n 9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-smith9799_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith9799-88">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The IRA also believed that the bombing campaign would tie down British soldiers in static positions guarding potential targets, preventing their deployment in <a href="/wiki/Counter-insurgency" title="Counter-insurgency">counter-insurgency</a> operations.<sup id="cite_ref-smith9799_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith9799-88">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Loyalist paramilitaries, including the UVF, carried out campaigns aimed at thwarting the IRA\'s aspirations and maintaining the political union with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Loyalist paramilitaries tended to target Catholics with no connection to the republican movement, seeking to undermine support for the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;n 10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-shanahanloyalists_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shanahanloyalists-96">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a result of escalating violence, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Demetrius" title="Operation Demetrius">internment without trial</a> was introduced by the Northern Ireland government on 9 August 1971, with 342 suspects arrested in the first twenty-four hours.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Despite loyalist violence also increasing, all of those arrested were republicans, including <a href="/wiki/Political_activist" class="mw-redirect" title="Political activist">political activists</a> not associated with the IRA and student civil rights leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-smith1971_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith1971-101">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloneyinternment_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyinternment-102">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The one-sided nature of internment united all Catholics in opposition to the government, and riots broke out in protest across Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-smith1971_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith1971-101">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-englishinternment_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-englishinternment-103">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Twenty-two people were killed in the next three days, including six civilians killed by the British Army in the <a href="/wiki/Ballymurphy_massacre" title="Ballymurphy massacre">Ballymurphy massacre</a> on 9 August,<sup id="cite_ref-moloneyinternment_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyinternment-102">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> and in Belfast 7,000 Catholics and 2,000 Protestants were forced from their homes by the rioting.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneyinternment_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyinternment-102">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The introduction of internment dramatically increased the level of violence, in the seven months prior to internment 34 people had been killed, 140 people were killed between the introduction of internment and the end of the year, including thirty soldiers and eleven RUC officers.<sup id="cite_ref-smith1971_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith1971-101">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloneyinternment_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyinternment-102">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Internment boosted IRA recruitment,<sup id="cite_ref-smith1971_101-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith1971-101">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and in Dublin the <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lynch" title="Jack Lynch">Jack Lynch</a>, abandoned a planned idea to introduce internment in the Republic of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;n 11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloneyinternment_102-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyinternment-102">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> IRA recruitment further increased after <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Sunday_(1972)" title="Bloody Sunday (1972)">Bloody Sunday</a> in Derry on 30 January 1972, when the British Army killed fourteen unarmed civilians during an anti-internment march.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Due to the deteriorating security situation in Northern Ireland the British government suspended the Northern Ireland parliament and imposed <a href="/wiki/Direct_rule_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Direct rule (Northern Ireland)">direct rule</a> in March 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The suspension of the Northern Ireland parliament was a key objective of the IRA, in order to directly involve the British government in Northern Ireland, as the IRA wanted the conflict to be seen as one between Ireland and Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-smith9799_88-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith9799-88">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In May 1972 the Official IRA called a ceasefire, leaving the Provisional IRA as the sole active republican paramilitary organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;n 12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sanders_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanders-114">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> New recruits saw the Official IRA as existing for the purpose of defence in contrast to the Provisional IRA as existing for the purpose of attack, increased recruitment and <a href="/wiki/Defection" title="Defection">defections</a> from the Official IRA to the Provisional IRA led to the latter becoming the dominant organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;n 13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_pub_bombings_plaque_Birmingham_England.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Birmingham_pub_bombings_plaque_Birmingham_England.jpg/220px-Birmingham_pub_bombings_plaque_Birmingham_England.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Birmingham_pub_bombings_plaque_Birmingham_England.jpg/330px-Birmingham_pub_bombings_plaque_Birmingham_England.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Birmingham_pub_bombings_plaque_Birmingham_England.jpg/440px-Birmingham_pub_bombings_plaque_Birmingham_England.jpg 2x" data-file-width="572" data-file-height="429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_pub_bombings_plaque_Birmingham_England.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial to the victims of the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_pub_bombings" title="Birmingham pub bombings">Birmingham pub bombings</a>, which killed twenty-one people in November 1974<sup id="cite_ref-birmingham_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birmingham-120">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>On 22 June the IRA announced that a ceasefire would begin at midnight on 26 June, in anticipation of talks with the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Two days later \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh and \xc3\x93 Conaill held a <a href="/wiki/Press_conference" title="Press conference">press conference</a> in Dublin to announce the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ire_Nua" title="\xc3\x89ire Nua">\xc3\x89ire Nua</a> (New Ireland) policy, which advocated an all-Ireland <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federal</a> republic, with <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved governments</a> and parliaments for each of the four historic <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Ireland" title="Provinces of Ireland">provinces of Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;n 14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-\xc3\xa9irenua_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc3\xa9irenua-126">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> This was designed to deal with the fears of unionists over a united Ireland, an <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> parliament with a narrow Protestant majority would provide them with protection for their interests.<sup id="cite_ref-\xc3\xa9irenua_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc3\xa9irenua-126">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The British government held secret talks with the republican leadership on 7 July, with Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in, \xc3\x93 Conaill, <a href="/wiki/Ivor_Bell" title="Ivor Bell">Ivor Bell</a>, Twomey, <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Adams" title="Gerry Adams">Gerry Adams</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Martin_McGuinness" title="Martin McGuinness">Martin McGuinness</a> flying to England to meet a British delegation led by <a href="/wiki/William_Whitelaw" class="mw-redirect" title="William Whitelaw">William Whitelaw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor1972_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1972-85">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in made demands including British withdrawal, removal of the British Army from sensitive areas, and a release of republican prisoners and an amnesty for fugitives.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor1972_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1972-85">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The British refused and the talks broke up, and the IRA\'s ceasefire ended on 9 July.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In late 1972 and early 1973 the IRA\'s leadership was being depleted by arrests on both sides of the Irish border, with Mac St\xc3\xadof\xc3\xa1in, \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh and McGuinness all imprisoned for IRA membership.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorengland_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorengland-129">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Due to the crisis <a href="/wiki/1973_Old_Bailey_bombing" title="1973 Old Bailey bombing">the IRA bombed London</a> in March 1973, as the Army Council believed bombs in England would have a greater impact on British public opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorengland_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorengland-129">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mcgladdery1970s_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgladdery1970s-130">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> This was followed by an intense period of IRA activity in England that left forty-five people dead by the end of 1974, including twenty-one civilians killed in the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_pub_bombings" title="Birmingham pub bombings">Birmingham pub bombings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-birmingham_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birmingham-120">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mcgladdery1970s_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgladdery1970s-130">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following an IRA ceasefire over the Christmas period in 1974 and a further one in January 1975, on 8 February the IRA issued a statement suspending "offensive military action" from six o\'clock the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-white1975a_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white1975a-132">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> A series of meetings took place between the IRA\'s leadership and British government representatives throughout the year, with the IRA being led to believe this was the start of a process of British withdrawal.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Occasional IRA violence occurred during the ceasefire, with bombs in Belfast, Derry, and South Armagh.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The IRA was also involved in <a href="/wiki/Tit_for_tat" title="Tit for tat">tit for tat</a> sectarian killings of Protestant civilians, in retaliation for sectarian killings by loyalist paramilitaries.<sup id="cite_ref-taylortruce_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylortruce-137">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloneyceasefire_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyceasefire-138">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> By July the Army Council was concerned at the progress of the talks, concluding there was no prospect of a lasting peace without a public declaration by the British government of their intent to withdraw from Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In August there was a gradual return to the armed campaign, and the truce effectively ended on 22 September when the IRA set off 22 bombs across Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-taylortruce_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylortruce-137">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/old_guard" class="extiw" title="wikt:old guard">old guard</a> leadership of \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh, \xc3\x93 Conaill, and McKee were criticised by a younger generation of activists following the ceasefire, and their influence in the IRA slowly declined.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylorng_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorng-142">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The younger generation viewed the ceasefire as being disastrous for the IRA, causing the organisation irreparable damage and taking it close to being defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorng_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorng-142">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The Army Council was accused of falling into a trap that allowed the British breathing space and time to build up <a href="/wiki/Military_intelligence" title="Military intelligence">intelligence</a> on the IRA, and McKee was criticised for allowing the IRA to become involved in sectarian killings, as well a feud with the Official IRA in October and November 1975 that left eleven people dead.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneyceasefire_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyceasefire-138">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="The_.22Long_War.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_&quot;Long_War&quot;">The "Long War"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The &quot;Long War&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/1981_Irish_hunger_strike" title="1981 Irish hunger strike">1981 Irish hunger strike</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armalite_and_ballot_box_strategy" title="Armalite and ballot box strategy">Armalite and ballot box strategy</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IRA_Resistance_Poster.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b0/IRA_Resistance_Poster.jpg/220px-IRA_Resistance_Poster.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b0/IRA_Resistance_Poster.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="205" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IRA_Resistance_Poster.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>IRA political poster from the 1980s, featuring a quote from <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Sands" title="Bobby Sands">Bobby Sands</a> written on the first day of the <a href="/wiki/1981_Irish_hunger_strike" title="1981 Irish hunger strike">1981 hunger strike</a><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Following the end of the ceasefire, the British government introduced a new three-part strategy to deal with the Troubles; the parts became known as <a href="/wiki/Ulsterisation" title="Ulsterisation">Ulsterisation</a>, normalisation, and criminalisation.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor202_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor202-144">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Ulsterisation involved increasing the role of the locally recruited RUC and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Defence_Regiment" title="Ulster Defence Regiment">Ulster Defence Regiment</a> (UDR), a part-time element of the British Army, in order to try to contain the conflict inside Northern Ireland and reduce the number of British soldiers recruited from outside of Northern Ireland being killed.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor202_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor202-144">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-white124_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white124-145">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Normalisation involved the ending of internment without trial and <a href="/wiki/Special_Category_Status" title="Special Category Status">Special Category Status</a>, the latter had been introduced in 1972 following a hunger strike led by McKee.<sup id="cite_ref-white124_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white124-145">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Criminalisation was designed to alter public perception of the Troubles, from an insurgency requiring a military solution to a criminal problem requiring a law enforcement solution.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor202_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor202-144">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> As result of the withdrawal of Special Category Status, in September 1976 IRA prisoner <a href="/wiki/Kieran_Nugent" title="Kieran Nugent">Kieran Nugent</a> began the <a href="/wiki/Blanket_protest" title="Blanket protest">blanket protest</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Maze_Prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Maze Prison">Maze Prison</a>, when hundreds of prisoners refused to wear prison uniforms.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1977 the IRA evolved a new strategy which they called the "Long War", which would remain their strategy for the rest of the Troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> This strategy accepted that their campaign would last many years before being successful, and included increased emphasis on political activity through Sinn F\xc3\xa9in.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorlw_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorlw-152">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> A republican document of the early 1980s states "Both Sinn F\xc3\xa9in and the IRA play different but converging roles in the war of national liberation. The Irish Republican Army wages an armed campaign<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... Sinn F\xc3\xa9in maintains the propaganda war and is the public and political voice of the movement".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The 1977 edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Green_Book_(IRA_training_manual)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Green Book (IRA training manual)">Green Book</a></i>, an induction and training manual used by the IRA, describes the strategy of the "Long War" in these terms:\n</p>\n<ol><li>A war of attrition against enemy personnel [British Army] which is aimed at causing as many casualties and deaths as possible so as to create a demand from their [the British] people at home for their withdrawal.</li>\n<li>A bombing campaign aimed at making the enemy\'s financial interests in our country unprofitable while at the same time curbing long-term investment in our country.</li>\n<li>To make the Six Counties<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... ungovernable except by colonial military rule.</li>\n<li>To sustain the war and gain support for its ends by National and International propaganda and publicity campaigns.</li>\n<li>By defending the war of liberation by punishing criminals, <a href="/wiki/Collaborationism" title="Collaborationism">collaborators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Informant" title="Informant">informers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>The "Long War" saw the IRA\'s tactics move away from the large bombing campaigns of the early 1970s, in favour of more attacks on members of the security forces.<sup id="cite_ref-smith_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith-156">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The IRA\'s new multi-faceted strategy saw them begin to use <a href="/wiki/Armed_propaganda" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed propaganda">armed propaganda</a>, using the publicity gained from attacks such as the assassination of <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mountbatten" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Mountbatten">Lord Mountbatten</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Warrenpoint_ambush" title="Warrenpoint ambush">Warrenpoint ambush</a> to focus attention on the nationalist community\'s rejection of British rule.<sup id="cite_ref-smith_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith-156">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The IRA aimed to keep Northern Ireland unstable, which would frustrate the British objective of installing a <a href="/wiki/Power_sharing" class="mw-redirect" title="Power sharing">power sharing</a> government as a solution to the Troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-smith_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith-156">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg/220px-Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg/330px-Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg/440px-Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1640" data-file-height="1011" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">Brighton hotel bombing</a>, an assassination attempt on British prime minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> in 1984<sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimerbrighton_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimerbrighton-157">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div><p>The prison protest against criminalisation culminated in the 1981 Irish hunger strike, when seven IRA and three <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a> members starved themselves to death in pursuit of political status.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The hunger strike leader <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Sands" title="Bobby Sands">Bobby Sands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti_H-Block" title="Anti H-Block">Anti H-Block</a> activist <a href="/wiki/Owen_Carron" title="Owen Carron">Owen Carron</a> were successively elected to the British <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons" title="House of Commons">House of Commons</a>, and two other protesting prisoners were elected to D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The electoral successes led to the IRA\'s armed campaign being pursued in parallel with increased electoral participation by Sinn F\xc3\xa9in.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> This strategy was known as the "Armalite and ballot box strategy", named after <a href="/wiki/Danny_Morrison_(Irish_republican)" title="Danny Morrison (Irish republican)">Danny Morrison</a>\'s speech at the 1981 Sinn F\xc3\xa9in ard fheis:\n</p><blockquote><p>Who here really believes that we can win the war through the ballot box? But will anyone here object if with a ballot paper in this hand and an Armalite in this hand we take power in Ireland?<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Attacks on high-profile political and military targets remained a priority for the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mcgladdery_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgladdery-163">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Barracks_bombing" title="Chelsea Barracks bombing">Chelsea Barracks bombing</a> in London in October 1981 killed two civilians and injured twenty-three soldiers; a week later the IRA struck again in London by an assassination attempt on Lieutenant General <a href="/wiki/Steuart_Pringle" title="Steuart Pringle">Steuart Pringle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commandant_General_Royal_Marines" title="Commandant General Royal Marines">Commandant General Royal Marines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mcgladdery_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgladdery-163">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Attacks on military targets in England continued with the <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park_and_Regent%27s_Park_bombings" title="Hyde Park and Regent&#39;s Park bombings">Hyde Park and Regent\'s Park bombings</a> in July 1982, which killed eleven soldiers and injured over fifty people including civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In October 1984 they carried out the <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">Brighton hotel bombing</a>, an assassination attempt on British prime minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>, whom they blamed for the deaths of the ten hunger strikers.<sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimerbrighton_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimerbrighton-157">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The bombing killed five members of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> attending a party conference including MP <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Berry" title="Anthony Berry">Anthony Berry</a>, with Thatcher narrowly escaping death.<sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimerbrighton_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimerbrighton-157">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> A planned escalation of the England bombing campaign in 1985 was prevented when six IRA volunteers, including <a href="/wiki/Martina_Anderson" title="Martina Anderson">Martina Anderson</a> and the Brighton bomber <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Magee_(Irish_republican)" title="Patrick Magee (Irish republican)">Patrick Magee</a>, were arrested in Glasgow.<sup id="cite_ref-engdept_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engdept-166">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Plans for a major escalation of the campaign in the late 1980s were cancelled after a ship carrying 150 tonnes of weapons donated by Libya was seized off the coast of France.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The plans, modelled on the <a href="/wiki/Tet_Offensive" title="Tet Offensive">Tet Offensive</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, relied on the element of surprise which was lost when the ship\'s captain informed French authorities of four earlier shipments of weapons, which allowed the British Army to deploy appropriate <a href="/wiki/Countermeasure" title="Countermeasure">countermeasures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In 1987 the IRA began attacking British military targets in mainland Europe, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/1987_Rheindahlen_bombing" title="1987 Rheindahlen bombing">Rheindahlen bombing</a>, which was followed by approximately twenty other gun and bomb attacks aimed at <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">British Armed Forces</a> personnel and bases between 1988 and 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-cooganeurope_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooganeurope-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_process">Peace process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Peace process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_peace_process" title="Northern Ireland peace process">Northern Ireland peace process</a></div>\n<p>By the late 1980s the Troubles were at a military and political stalemate, with the IRA able to prevent the British government imposing a settlement but unable to force their objective of Irish reunification.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Sinn F\xc3\xa9in president Adams was in contact with <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_and_Labour_Party" title="Social Democratic and Labour Party">Social Democratic and Labour Party</a> (SDLP) leader <a href="/wiki/John_Hume" title="John Hume">John Hume</a> and a delegation representing the Irish government, in order to find political alternatives to the IRA\'s campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the republican leadership appearing interested in peace, British policy shifted when <a href="/wiki/Peter_Brooke" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Brooke">Peter Brooke</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Secretary of State for Northern Ireland">Secretary of State for Northern Ireland</a>, began to engage with them hoping for a political settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-niall_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-niall-172">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Track_II_diplomacy" title="Track II diplomacy">Backchannel diplomacy</a> between the IRA and British government began in October 1990, with Sinn F\xc3\xa9in being given an advance copy of a planned speech by Brooke.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The speech was given in London the following month, with Brooke stating that the British government would not give in to violence but offering significant political change if violence stopped, ending his statement by saying:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The British government has no selfish, strategic or economic interest in Northern Ireland: Our role is to help, enable and encourage <span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... Partition is an acknowledgement of reality, not an assertion of national self-interest.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;n 15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-o&#39;brienbrooke_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-o&#39;brienbrooke-178">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sniperatwork.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Sniperatwork.jpg/220px-Sniperatwork.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Sniperatwork.jpg/330px-Sniperatwork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Sniperatwork.jpg 2x" data-file-width="409" data-file-height="268" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sniperatwork.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "Sniper at Work" sign in <a href="/wiki/Crossmaglen" title="Crossmaglen">Crossmaglen</a>. The IRA\'s <a href="/wiki/South_Armagh_Brigade" class="mw-redirect" title="South Armagh Brigade">South Armagh Brigade</a> killed seven members of the security forces in <a href="/wiki/South_Armagh_Sniper_(1990%E2%80%931997)" title="South Armagh Sniper (1990\xe2\x80\x931997)">single-shot sniper attacks</a> in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The IRA responded to Brooke\'s speech by declaring a three-day ceasefire over Christmas, the first in fifteen years.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Afterwards the IRA intensified the bombing campaign in England, planting 36 bombs in 1991 and 57 in 1992, up from 15 in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Exchange_bombing" title="Baltic Exchange bombing">Baltic Exchange bombing</a> in April 1992 killed three people and caused an estimated \xc2\xa3800&#160;million worth of damage, \xc2\xa3200&#160;million more than the total damage caused by the Troubles in Northern Ireland up to that point.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> In December 1992 <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Mayhew" title="Patrick Mayhew">Patrick Mayhew</a>, who had succeeded Brooke as Secretary of State for Northern Ireland, gave a speech directed at the IRA in <a href="/wiki/Coleraine" title="Coleraine">Coleraine</a>, stating that while Irish reunification could be achieved by negotiation, the British government would not give in to violence.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The secret talks between the British government and the IRA via <a href="/wiki/Intermediary" title="Intermediary">intermediaries</a> continued, with the British government arguing the IRA would be more likely to achieve its objective through politics than continued violence.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;n 16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylorpeace_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorpeace-186">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The talks progressed slowly due to continued IRA violence, including the <a href="/wiki/Warrington_bombings" title="Warrington bombings">Warrington bombing</a> in March 1993 which killed two children and the <a href="/wiki/Bishopsgate_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishopsgate bombing">Bishopsgate bombing</a> a month later which killed one person and caused an estimated \xc2\xa31&#160;billion worth of damage.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> In December 1993 a press conference was held at London\'s <a href="/wiki/Downing_Street" title="Downing Street">Downing Street</a> by British prime minister <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> and the Irish Taoiseach <a href="/wiki/Albert_Reynolds" title="Albert Reynolds">Albert Reynolds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> They delivered the <a href="/wiki/Downing_Street_Declaration" title="Downing Street Declaration">Downing Street Declaration</a> which conceded the right of Irish people to <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a>, but with separate referendums in Northern Ireland and the Republic of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> In January 1994 The Army Council voted to reject the declaration, while Sinn F\xc3\xa9in asked the British government to clarify certain aspects of the declaration.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> The British government replied saying the declaration spoke for itself, and refused to meet with Sinn F\xc3\xa9in unless the IRA called a ceasefire.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 31 August 1994 the IRA announced a "complete cessation of military operations" on the understanding that Sinn F\xc3\xa9in would be included in political talks for a settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> A new strategy known as "TUAS" was revealed to the IRA\'s rank-and-file following the ceasefire, described as either "Tactical Use of Armed Struggle" to the <a href="/wiki/Republican_movement_(Ireland)" title="Republican movement (Ireland)">Irish republican movement</a> or "Totally Unarmed Strategy" to the broader Irish nationalist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneytuas_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneytuas-195">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The strategy involved a coalition including Sinn F\xc3\xa9in, the SDLP and the Irish government acting in concert to apply leverage to the British government, with the IRA\'s armed campaign starting and stopping as necessary, and an option to call off the ceasefire if negotiations failed.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneytuas_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneytuas-195">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> The British government refused to admit
