Sinn F\xc3\xa9in to multi-party talks before the IRA <a href="/wiki/Decommissioning_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Decommissioning in Northern Ireland">decommissioned its weapons</a>, and a standoff began as the IRA refused to disarm before a final peace settlement had been agreed.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorcf_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorcf-197">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The IRA regarded themselves as being undefeated and decommissioning as an act of surrender, and stated decommissioning had never been mentioned prior to the ceasefire being declared.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorcf_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorcf-197">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In March 1995 Mayhew set out three conditions for Sinn F\xc3\xa9in being admitted to multi-party talks.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorcf_197-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorcf-197">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Firstly the IRA had to be willing to agree to "disarm progressively", secondly a scheme for decommissioning had to be agreed, and finally some weapons had to be decommissioned prior to the talks beginning as a <a href="/wiki/Confidence_building_measure" class="mw-redirect" title="Confidence building measure">confidence building measure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorcf_197-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorcf-197">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The IRA responded with public statements in September calling decommissioning an "unreasonable demand" and a "<a href="/wiki/Delaying_tactic" title="Delaying tactic">stalling tactic</a>" by the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1996_Docklands_bombing_plaque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1996_Docklands_bombing_plaque.jpg/220px-1996_Docklands_bombing_plaque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1996_Docklands_bombing_plaque.jpg/330px-1996_Docklands_bombing_plaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1996_Docklands_bombing_plaque.jpg/440px-1996_Docklands_bombing_plaque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="591" data-file-height="432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1996_Docklands_bombing_plaque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial to the victims of the <a href="/wiki/1996_Docklands_bombing" title="1996 Docklands bombing">1996 Docklands bombing</a>, which killed two people and ended the IRA\'s seventeen-month ceasefire<sup id="cite_ref-docklands_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-docklands-199">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>On 9 February 1996 a statement from the Army Council was delivered to the Irish national broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Raidi%C3%B3_Teilif%C3%ADs_%C3%89ireann" title="Raidi\xc3\xb3 Teilif\xc3\xads \xc3\x89ireann">Raidi\xc3\xb3 Teilif\xc3\xads \xc3\x89ireann</a> announcing the end of the ceasefire, and just over 90 minutes later the <a href="/wiki/Docklands_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Docklands bombing">Docklands bombing</a> killed two people and caused an estimated \xc2\xa3100\xe2\x80\x93150&#160;million damage to some of London\'s more expensive <a href="/wiki/Commercial_property" title="Commercial property">commercial property</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-docklands_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-docklands-199">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Three weeks later the British and Irish governments issued a joint statement announcing multi-party talks would begin on 10 June, with Sinn F\xc3\xa9in excluded unless the IRA called a new ceasefire.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor1996_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1996-201">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The IRA\'s campaign continued with the <a href="/wiki/1996_Manchester_bombing" title="1996 Manchester bombing">Manchester bombing</a> on 15 June, which injured over 200 people and caused an estimated \xc2\xa3400&#160;million of damage to the city centre.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Attacks were mostly in England apart from the <a href="/wiki/Osnabr%C3%BCck_mortar_attack" title="Osnabr\xc3\xbcck mortar attack">Osnabr\xc3\xbcck mortar attack</a> on a British Army base in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor1996_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1996-201">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The IRA\'s first attack in Northern Ireland since the end of the ceasefire was not until October 1996, when the <a href="/wiki/Thiepval_barracks_bombing" title="Thiepval barracks bombing">Thiepval barracks bombing</a> killed a British soldier.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> In February 1997 an <a href="/wiki/South_Armagh_Sniper_(1990%E2%80%931997)" title="South Armagh Sniper (1990\xe2\x80\x931997)">IRA sniper team</a> killed <a href="/wiki/Lance_Bombadier" class="mw-redirect" title="Lance Bombadier">Lance Bombadier</a> Stephen Restorick, the last British soldier to be killed by the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/1997_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1997 United Kingdom general election">May 1997 UK general election</a> Major was replaced as prime minister by <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-moloney1997_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloney1997-206">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The new Secretary of State for Northern Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Mo_Mowlam" title="Mo Mowlam">Mo Mowlam</a>, had announced prior to the election she would be willing to include Sinn F\xc3\xa9in in multi-party talks without prior decommissioning of weapons within two months of an IRA ceasefire.<sup id="cite_ref-moloney1997_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloney1997-206">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> After the IRA declared a new ceasefire in July 1997, Sinn F\xc3\xa9in was admitted into multi-party talks, which produced the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a> in April 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor354_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor354-207">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-english297_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-english297-208">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> One aim of the agreement was that all paramilitary groups in Northern Ireland fully disarm by May 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> The IRA began decommissioning in a process that was monitored by Canadian General <a href="/wiki/John_de_Chastelain" title="John de Chastelain">John de Chastelain</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Independent_International_Commission_on_Decommissioning" title="Independent International Commission on Decommissioning">Independent International Commission on Decommissioning</a> (IICD),<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> with some weapons being decommissioned on 23 October 2001 and 8 April 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> The October 2001 decommissioning was the first time an Irish republican paramilitary organisation had voluntarily disposed of its arms.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;n 17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-boyne403_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne403-212">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> In October 2002 the devolved <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> was suspended by the British government and direct rule returned, in order to prevent a unionist walkout.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;n 18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> This was partly triggered by <a href="/wiki/Stormontgate" title="Stormontgate">Stormontgate</a>\xe2\x80\x94allegations that republican spies were operating within the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Buildings_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Parliament Buildings (Northern Ireland)">Parliament Buildings</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Service of Northern Ireland">Police Service of Northern Ireland</a> (PSNI)<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;n 19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94and the IRA temporarily broke off contact with de Chastelain.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> However, further decommissioning took place on 21 October 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> In the aftermath of the December 2004 <a href="/wiki/Northern_Bank_robbery" title="Northern Bank robbery">Northern Bank robbery</a>, the Minister for Justice, Equality and Law Reform <a href="/wiki/Michael_McDowell_(politician)" title="Michael McDowell (politician)">Michael McDowell</a> stated there could be no place in government in either Northern Ireland or the Republic of Ireland for a party that supported or threatened the use of violence, possessed explosives or firearms, and was involved in criminality.<sup id="cite_ref-boyne_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne-222">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> At the beginning of February 2005, the IRA declared that it was withdrawing a decommissioning offer from late 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-boyne_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne-222">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> This followed a demand from the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionist Party</a>, under Paisley, insisting on photographic evidence of decommissioning.<sup id="cite_ref-boyne_222-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne-222">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_armed_campaign">End of the armed campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: End of the armed campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army_actions_(2000%E2%80%9309)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chronology of Provisional Irish Republican Army actions (2000\xe2\x80\x9309)">Chronology of Provisional Irish Republican Army actions (2000\xe2\x80\x9309)</a></div>\n<p>On 28 July 2005 the IRA announced an end to the armed campaign, stating that it would work to achieve its aims solely by peaceful political means, with volunteers to end all paramilitary activity.<sup id="cite_ref-boyne408_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne408-223">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The IRA also stated it would complete the process of disarmament as quickly as possible.<sup id="cite_ref-boyne408_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne408-223">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The IRA invited two independent witnesses to view the secret disarmament work, Catholic priest Father <a href="/wiki/Alec_Reid" title="Alec Reid">Alec Reid</a> and Protestant minister Reverend <a href="/wiki/Harold_Good" title="Harold Good">Harold Good</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> On 26 September 2005, the IICD announced that "the totality of the IRA\'s arsenal" had been decommissioned.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Information_Group" title="Jane&#39;s Information Group">Jane\'s Information Group</a> estimated the weaponry decommissioned in September 2005 included:\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kongsberg_AG-3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Kongsberg_AG-3.jpg/220px-Kongsberg_AG-3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="51" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Kongsberg_AG-3.jpg/330px-Kongsberg_AG-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Kongsberg_AG-3.jpg/440px-Kongsberg_AG-3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2720" data-file-height="630" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kongsberg_AG-3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An AG-3, Norwegian made variant of the <a href="/wiki/Heckler_%26_Koch_G3" title="Heckler &amp; Koch G3">Heckler &amp; Koch G3</a>. Over 50 of these, from a batch of 100 stolen from the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Army" title="Norwegian Army">Norwegian Army</a>, ended up with the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RPG-7_detached.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/RPG-7_detached.jpg/220px-RPG-7_detached.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/RPG-7_detached.jpg/330px-RPG-7_detached.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/RPG-7_detached.jpg/440px-RPG-7_detached.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RPG-7_detached.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/RPG-7" title="RPG-7">RPG-7</a>, first obtained by the IRA from Libya in 1972<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<ul><li>1,000 rifles</li>\n<li>2 tonnes of the plastic explosive <a href="/wiki/Semtex" title="Semtex">Semtex</a></li>\n<li>20\xe2\x80\x9330 <a href="/wiki/Heavy_machine_gun" title="Heavy machine gun">heavy machine guns</a></li>\n<li>7 <a href="/wiki/Man-portable_air-defense_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Man-portable air-defense systems">surface-to-air missiles</a></li>\n<li>7 <a href="/wiki/Flamethrower" title="Flamethrower">flamethrowers</a></li>\n<li>1,200 <a href="/wiki/Detonator" title="Detonator">detonators</a></li>\n<li>11 <a href="/wiki/Rocket-propelled_grenade" title="Rocket-propelled grenade">rocket-propelled grenade</a> launchers</li>\n<li>90 <a href="/wiki/Handgun" title="Handgun">handguns</a></li>\n<li>100+ <a href="/wiki/Hand_grenade" class="mw-redirect" title="Hand grenade">hand grenades</a><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Having compared the weapons decommissioned with the British and Irish security forces\' estimates of the IRA\'s arsenal, and because of the IRA\'s full involvement in the process of decommissioning the weapons, the IICD arrived at their conclusion that all IRA weaponry has been decommissioned.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;n 20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> The Secretary of State for Northern Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hain" title="Peter Hain">Peter Hain</a>, said he accepted the conclusion of the IICD.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Since then, there have been occasional claims in the media that the IRA had not decommissioned all of its weaponry.<sup id="cite_ref-boyne414_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne414-235">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> In response to such claims, the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Monitoring_Commission" title="Independent Monitoring Commission">Independent Monitoring Commission</a> (IMC) stated in its tenth report that the IRA had decommissioned all weaponry under its control.<sup id="cite_ref-boyne414_235-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne414-235">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> It said that if any weapons had been kept, they would have been kept by individuals and against IRA orders.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;n 21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-boyne414_235-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyne414-235">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2015 <a href="/wiki/Garda_Commissioner" title="Garda Commissioner">Garda Commissioner</a> <a href="/wiki/N%C3%B3ir%C3%ADn_O%27Sullivan" title="N\xc3\xb3ir\xc3\xadn O&#39;Sullivan">N\xc3\xb3ir\xc3\xadn O\'Sullivan</a> stated that the Republic of Ireland\'s police service, the <a href="/wiki/Garda%C3%AD" class="mw-redirect" title="Garda\xc3\xad">Garda\xc3\xad</a>, have no evidence that the IRA\'s military structure remains or that the IRA is engaged in crime.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> In August 2015 <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton_(police_officer)" title="George Hamilton (police officer)">George Hamilton</a>, the PSNI <a href="/wiki/Chief_constable" title="Chief constable">chief constable</a>, stated that the IRA no longer exists as a paramilitary organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-psni2015_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psni2015-240">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> He said that some of its structure remains, but that the group is committed to following a peaceful political path and is not engaged in criminal activity or directing violence.<sup id="cite_ref-psni2015_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psni2015-240">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> However, he added that some members have engaged in criminal activity or violence for their own ends.<sup id="cite_ref-psni2015_240-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psni2015-240">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> The statement was in response to the recent killings of two former IRA members.<sup id="cite_ref-psni2015_240-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psni2015-240">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> In August Kevin McGuigan was shot dead, believed to be a <a href="/wiki/Revenge_killing" class="mw-redirect" title="Revenge killing">revenge killing</a> by former IRA members over the shooting death of former Belfast IRA commander <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Davison" title="Gerard Davison">Gerard Davison</a> three months earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> The chief constable stated there was no evidence that the killing of McGuigan was sanctioned by the IRA leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-psni2015_240-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psni2015-240">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> In response, the British government commissioned the <a href="/wiki/Assessment_on_Paramilitary_Groups_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Assessment on Paramilitary Groups in Northern Ireland">Assessment on Paramilitary Groups in Northern Ireland</a>, which concluded in October 2015 that the IRA, while committed to peace, continued to exist in a reduced form.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Weaponry_and_operations">Weaponry and operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Weaponry and operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_arms_importation" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional IRA arms importation">Provisional IRA arms importation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_campaign_1969-1997" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional IRA campaign 1969-1997">Provisional IRA campaign 1969-1997</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_chronologies_of_Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army_actions" title="List of chronologies of Provisional Irish Republican Army actions">List of chronologies of Provisional Irish Republican Army actions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barrack_buster" title="Barrack buster">Barrack buster</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Improvised_tactical_vehicles_of_the_Provisional_IRA" title="Improvised tactical vehicles of the Provisional IRA">Improvised tactical vehicles of the Provisional IRA</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Armalite_18-IMG_6519-white.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Armalite_18-IMG_6519-white.jpg/220px-Armalite_18-IMG_6519-white.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Armalite_18-IMG_6519-white.jpg/330px-Armalite_18-IMG_6519-white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Armalite_18-IMG_6519-white.jpg/440px-Armalite_18-IMG_6519-white.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Armalite_18-IMG_6519-white.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Armalite_AR-18" class="mw-redirect" title="Armalite AR-18">Armalite AR-18</a>, obtained by the IRA from the United States in the early 1970s, was a symbol of its armed campaign<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In the early days of <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">the Troubles</a> the IRA was poorly armed, in Derry in early 1972 the IRA\'s weaponry consisted of six <a href="/wiki/M1_carbine" title="M1 carbine">M1 carbines</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Thompson_submachine_gun" title="Thompson submachine gun">Thompson submachine guns</a>, one or two <a href="/wiki/M1_Garand" title="M1 Garand">M1 Garand</a> rifles, and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Handgun" title="Handgun">handguns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> As a result of black market arms deals and donations from sympathisers, the IRA obtained a large array of weapons such as <a href="/wiki/Surface-to-air_missiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Surface-to-air missiles">surface-to-air missiles</a>; <a href="/wiki/M60_machine_gun" title="M60 machine gun">M60 machine guns</a>; <a href="/wiki/ArmaLite_AR-18" title="ArmaLite AR-18">ArmaLite AR-18</a>, <a href="/wiki/FN_FAL" title="FN FAL">FN FAL</a>, <a href="/wiki/AKM" title="AKM">AKM</a> and <a href="/wiki/M16_rifle" title="M16 rifle">M16 rifles</a>; <a href="/wiki/DShK" title="DShK">DShK</a> heavy machine guns; <a href="/wiki/LPO-50" title="LPO-50">LPO-50</a> flamethrowers; and <a href="/wiki/Barrett_M90" title="Barrett M90">Barrett M90</a> sniper rifles.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> The IRA also used a variety of bombs during its armed campaign, such as <a href="/wiki/Car_bomb" title="Car bomb">car and truck bombs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Time_bomb" title="Time bomb">time bombs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Booby_trap" title="Booby trap">booby traps</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimer51_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimer51-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> using explosives including <a href="/wiki/ANFO" title="ANFO">ANFO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gelignite" title="Gelignite">gelignite</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Plastic_explosive" title="Plastic explosive">plastic explosive</a> Semtex.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> The IRA\'s engineering department also manufactured <a href="/wiki/Barrack_buster" title="Barrack buster">a series of improvised mortars</a>, which by the 1990s were built to a standard comparable to military models.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> The IRA\'s development of mortar tactics was a response to the heavy fortifications on RUC and British Army bases, as IRA mortars generally <a href="/wiki/Indirect_fire" title="Indirect fire">fired indirectly</a> they were able to bypass some <a href="/wiki/Perimeter_security" title="Perimeter security">perimeter security</a> measures.<sup id="cite_ref-mortar_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mortar-251">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> The mortars used a variety of different <a href="/wiki/Firing_mechanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Firing mechanism">firing mechanisms</a> including delay timers, this combined with the disposable nature of the weapons allowed IRA volunteers to reduce the risk of being arrested at the scene.<sup id="cite_ref-mortar_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mortar-251">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The IRA was mainly active in Northern Ireland, although it also attacked targets in England and mainland Europe, and limited activity also took place in the Republic of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-bowyerbellengland_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowyerbellengland-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cooganeurope_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooganeurope-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> The IRA\'s offensive campaign mainly targeted the British Army (including the UDR) and the RUC, with British soldiers being the IRA\'s preferred target.<sup id="cite_ref-targets_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-targets-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Other targets included British government officials, politicians, <a href="/wiki/The_Establishment" title="The Establishment">establishment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judicial" class="mw-redirect" title="Judicial">judicial</a> figures, and senior British Army and police officers.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> The bombing campaign principally targeted political, economic and military targets, and was described by counter-terrorism expert <a href="/wiki/Andy_Oppenheimer" title="Andy Oppenheimer">Andy Oppenheimer</a> as "the biggest terrorist bombing campaign in history".<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Economic targets included shops, restaurants, hotels, railway stations and other public buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimer51_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimer51-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> The IRA was blamed for the <a href="/wiki/Abercorn_Restaurant_bombing" title="Abercorn Restaurant bombing">Abercorn Restaurant bombing</a> in March 1972, when a bomb exploded without warning killing two women and injuring many people.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;n 22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abercorn_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abercorn-259">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Due to negative publicity after the Abercorn bombing, the IRA introduced a system of telephoned coded warnings to try and avoid civilian casualties while still causing the intended damage to properties and the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;n 23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Civilian deaths were counter-productive to the IRA, as they provided the British with <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> coups and affected recruitment and funding.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Despite this IRA bombs continued to kill civilians, generally due to IRA mistakes and incompetence or errors in communication.<sup id="cite_ref-bowyerbell87_260-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowyerbell87-260">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-coogan1972_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan1972-268">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> These included the <a href="/wiki/Donegall_Street_bombing" title="Donegall Street bombing">Donegall Street bombing</a> which killed seven people including four civilians, and <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Friday_(1972)" title="Bloody Friday (1972)">Bloody Friday</a>, when nine people, five of them civilians, were killed when twenty-two bombs were planted in a one-mile radius of Belfast city centre.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan1972_268-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan1972-268">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Premature explosions were another cause of civilian deaths, such as the <a href="/wiki/Remembrance_Day_bombing" title="Remembrance Day bombing">Remembrance Day bombing</a> which killed eleven people including ten civilians,<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Shankill_Road_bombing" title="Shankill Road bombing">Shankill Road bombing</a> which killed ten people including eight civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Casualties">Casualties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Casualties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For a detailed breakdown of casualties caused by and inflicted on the IRA, see <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_campaign_1969-1997#Casualties" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional IRA campaign 1969-1997">Provisional IRA campaign 1969-1997 \xc2\xa7&#160;Casualties</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bogside_(18),_August_2009.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Bogside_%2818%29%2C_August_2009.JPG/220px-Bogside_%2818%29%2C_August_2009.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Bogside_%2818%29%2C_August_2009.JPG/330px-Bogside_%2818%29%2C_August_2009.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Bogside_%2818%29%2C_August_2009.JPG/440px-Bogside_%2818%29%2C_August_2009.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bogside_(18),_August_2009.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial to members of the IRA\'s <a href="/wiki/Derry_Brigade" class="mw-redirect" title="Derry Brigade">Derry Brigade</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The IRA was responsible for more deaths than any other organisation during the Troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Two detailed studies of deaths in the Troubles, the <a href="/wiki/Conflict_Archive_on_the_Internet" title="Conflict Archive on the Internet">Conflict Archive on the Internet</a> (CAIN), and the book <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_Lives" title="Lost Lives">Lost Lives</a></i>, differ slightly on the numbers killed by the IRA and the total number of conflict deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-quillgan_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quillgan-274">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> According to CAIN, the IRA was responsible for 1,705 deaths, about 48% of the total conflict deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> Of these, 1,009 (about 59%) were members or former members of the British security forces, while 508 (about 29%) were civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-crosstabs_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crosstabs-276">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> According to <i>Lost Lives</i>, the IRA was responsible for 1,781 deaths, about 47% of the total conflict deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-ll1536_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ll1536-277">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Of these, 944 (about 53%) were members of the British security forces, while 644 (about 36%) were civilians (including 61 former members of the security forces).<sup id="cite_ref-ll1536_277-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ll1536-277">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> The civilian figure also includes civilians employed by British security forces, politicians, members of the judiciary, and alleged criminals and <a href="/wiki/Informant" title="Informant">informers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ll1536_277-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ll1536-277">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Most of the remainder were loyalist or republican paramilitary members, including over 100 IRA members accidentally killed by their own bombs or shot for being security force agents or informers.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Overall, the IRA was responsible for 87\xe2\x80\x9390% of the total British security force deaths, and 27\xe2\x80\x9330% of the total civilian deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-crosstabs_276-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crosstabs-276">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ll1536_277-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ll1536-277">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> During the IRA\'s campaign in England it was responsible for at least 488 incidents causing 2,134 injuries and 115 deaths, including 56 civilians and 42 British soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;n 24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Between 275 and 300 IRA members were killed during the Troubles,<sup id="cite_ref-ll1531_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ll1531-285">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-suttonstatus_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonstatus-286">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> with the IRA\'s biggest loss of life in a single incident being the <a href="/wiki/Loughgall_ambush" title="Loughgall ambush">Loughgall ambush</a> in 1987, when eight volunteers attempting to bomb a police station were killed by the British Army\'s <a href="/wiki/Special_Air_Service" title="Special Air Service">Special Air Service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PIRAcolourpartyDublin.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6f/PIRAcolourpartyDublin.JPG/220px-PIRAcolourpartyDublin.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6f/PIRAcolourpartyDublin.JPG/330px-PIRAcolourpartyDublin.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6f/PIRAcolourpartyDublin.JPG/440px-PIRAcolourpartyDublin.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PIRAcolourpartyDublin.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Republican colour party in Dublin, March 2009. The blue flag being carried at the front is that of "Dublin Brigade IRA".</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/IRA_Army_Council" title="IRA Army Council">IRA Army Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/IRA_Northern_Command" title="IRA Northern Command">IRA Northern Command</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Active_service_unit" title="Active service unit">Active service unit</a></div>\n<p>All levels of the organisation were entitled to send delegates to General Army Conventions.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The convention was the IRA\'s supreme decision-making authority, and was supposed to meet every two years,<sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> or every four years following a change to the IRA\'s constitution in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;n 25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-constitution_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Before 1969 conventions met regularly, but owing to the difficulty in organising such a large gathering of an illegal organisation in secret,<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;n 26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gac_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gac-292">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> while the IRA\'s armed campaign was ongoing they were only held in September 1970,<sup id="cite_ref-gac_292-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gac-292">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> October 1986,<sup id="cite_ref-gac_292-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gac-292">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> and October or November 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> After the 1997 ceasefire they were held more frequently, and are known to have been held in October 1997,<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> May 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> December 1998 or early 1999,<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> and June 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> The convention elected a 12-member Executive, which selected seven members, usually from within the Executive, to form the Army Council.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;n 27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-structure_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-structure-303">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> Any vacancies on the Executive would then be filled by substitutes previously elected by the convention.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> For day-to-day purposes, authority was vested in the Army Council which, as well as directing policy and taking major tactical decisions, appointed a <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the Irish Republican Army">chief-of-staff</a> from one of its number or, less often, from outside its ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The chief-of-staff would be assisted by an <a href="/wiki/Adjutant_general" title="Adjutant general">adjutant general</a> as well as a General Headquarters (GHQ) staff, which consisted of a <a href="/wiki/IRA_Quartermaster_General" title="IRA Quartermaster General">quartermaster general</a>, and directors of finance, engineering, training, intelligence, publicity, operations, and security.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-structure_303-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-structure-303">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> GHQ\'s largest department, the quartermaster general\'s, accounted for approximately 20% of the IRA\'s personnel, and was responsible for acquiring weapons and smuggling them to Ireland where they would be hidden in arms dumps, and distributed them to IRA units as needed.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The next most important department was engineering, which manufactured <a href="/wiki/Improvised_explosive_device" title="Improvised explosive device">improvised explosive devices</a> and improvised mortars.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Below GHQ, the IRA was divided into a Northern Command and a Southern Command.<sup id="cite_ref-structure_303-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-structure-303">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> Northern Command operated in Northern Ireland as well as the <a href="/wiki/Irish_border" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish border">border</a> counties of <a href="/wiki/County_Donegal" title="County Donegal">Donegal</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Leitrim" title="County Leitrim">Leitrim</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Cavan" title="County Cavan">Cavan</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Monaghan" title="County Monaghan">Monaghan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/County_Louth" title="County Louth">Louth</a>, while Southern Command operated in the remainder of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-northerncommand_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northerncommand-306">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> In 1977, parallel to the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Clandestine_cell_system" title="Clandestine cell system">cell structures</a> at the local level, command of the "war-zone" was given to the Northern Command, which facilitated coordinated attacks across Northern Ireland and rapid alterations in tactics.<sup id="cite_ref-northerncommand_306-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northerncommand-306">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> Southern Command consisted of the Dublin Brigade and a number of smaller units in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-structure_303-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-structure-303">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> Its main responsibilities were support activities for Northern Command, such as importation and storage of arms, providing <a href="/wiki/Safe_house" title="Safe house">safe houses</a>, raising funds through robberies, and organising <a href="/wiki/Training_camp" title="Training camp">training camps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> Another department attached to GHQ but separate from all other IRA structures was the England department, responsible for the bombing campaign in England.<sup id="cite_ref-engdept_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engdept-166">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloney29_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloney29-309">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The IRA referred to its ordinary members as volunteers (or <i>\xc3\xb3glaigh</i> in Irish), to reflect the IRA being an <a href="/wiki/Irregular_army" class="mw-redirect" title="Irregular army">irregular army</a> which people were not forced to join and could leave at any time.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> Until the late 1970s, IRA volunteers were organised in units based on conventional military structures.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> Volunteers living in one area formed a <a href="/wiki/Company_(military_unit)" title="Company (military unit)">company</a> as part of a <a href="/wiki/Battalion" title="Battalion">battalion</a>, which could be part of a <a href="/wiki/Brigade" title="Brigade">brigade</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_Belfast_Brigade" title="Provisional IRA Belfast Brigade">Belfast Brigade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_Derry_Brigade" title="Provisional IRA Derry Brigade">Derry Brigade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_South_Armagh_Brigade" title="Provisional IRA South Armagh Brigade">South Armagh Brigade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Tyrone_Brigade" class="mw-redirect" title="East Tyrone Brigade">East Tyrone Brigade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> In late 1973 the Belfast Brigade restructured, introducing clandestine cells named <a href="/wiki/Active_service_unit" title="Active service unit">active service units</a>, consisting of between four and ten members.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> Similar changes were made elsewhere in the IRA by 1977, moving away from the larger conventional military organisational principle owing to its security vulnerability.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> The old structures were used for support activities such as policing nationalist areas, <a href="/wiki/Human_intelligence_(intelligence_gathering)" title="Human intelligence (intelligence gathering)">intelligence-gathering</a>, and hiding weapons,<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> while the bulk of attacks were carried out by active service units, using weapons controlled by the brigade\'s <a href="/wiki/Quartermaster" title="Quartermaster">quartermaster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-structure_303-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-structure-303">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> The exception to this reorganisation was the South Armagh Brigade, which retained its traditional hierarchy and battalion structure.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneygac_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneygac-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Only a handful of volunteers from the South Armagh Brigade were convicted of serious offences, and it had fewer arrests than any other area, meaning that the security forces struggled to recruit informers.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;n 28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harnden34\xe2\x80\x9335_320-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harnden34\xe2\x80\x9335-320">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_ideology">Political ideology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Political ideology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tommy_McKearney_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Tommy_McKearney_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Tommy_McKearney_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Tommy_McKearney_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Tommy_McKearney_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Tommy_McKearney_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Tommy_McKearney_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="3320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tommy_McKearney_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Former IRA volunteer <a href="/wiki/Tommy_McKearney" title="Tommy McKearney">Tommy McKearney</a>, who left the IRA in 1986 and formed the League of Communist Republicans<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The IRA\'s goal was an all-Ireland <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialist</a> republic.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_English" title="Richard English">Richard English</a>, a professor at <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University_Belfast" title="Queen&#39;s University Belfast">Queen\'s University Belfast</a>, writes that while the IRA\'s adherence to socialist goals has varied according to time and place, radical ideas, specifically socialist ones, were a key part of IRA thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-socialism_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-socialism-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Former IRA volunteer <a href="/wiki/Tommy_McKearney" title="Tommy McKearney">Tommy McKearney</a> states that while the IRA\'s goal was a socialist republic, there was no coherent analysis or understanding of socialism itself, other than an idea that the details would be worked out following an IRA victory.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> This was in contrast to the Official IRA and the Irish National Liberation Army, both of which adopted clearly defined <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> positions.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the Northern Ireland left-wing politician <a href="/wiki/Eamonn_McCann" title="Eamonn McCann">Eamonn McCann</a> has remarked that the Provisional IRA was considered a non-socialist IRA compared to the Official IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1980s, the IRA\'s commitment to socialism became more solidified as IRA prisoners began to engage with works of political and <a href="/wiki/Marxist_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist theory">Marxist theory</a> by authors such as <a href="/wiki/Frantz_Fanon" title="Frantz Fanon">Frantz Fanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara" title="Che Guevara">Che Guevara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Gramsci" title="Antonio Gramsci">Antonio Gramsci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ho-Chi_Minh" class="mw-redirect" title="Ho-Chi Minh">Ho-Chi Minh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/General_Giap" class="mw-redirect" title="General Giap">General Giap</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-reinisch_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reinisch-327">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> Members felt that an Irish version of the Tet Offensive could possibly be the key to victory against the British, pending on the arrival of weapons secured from Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-reinisch_327-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reinisch-327">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> However, this never came to pass, and the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall#Fall_of_the_Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">fall of the Berlin wall</a> in 1989 brought a dogmatic commitment to socialism back into question, as possible socialist allies in Eastern Europe wilted away.<sup id="cite_ref-reinisch_327-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reinisch-327">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> In the years that followed, IRA prisoners began to look towards South African politics and the example being set by the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-reinisch_327-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reinisch-327">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> Many of the imprisoned IRA members saw parallels between their own struggle and that of <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> and were encouraged by Mandela\'s use of compromise following his ascent to power in South Africa to consider compromise themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-reinisch_327-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reinisch-327">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Categorisation">Categorisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Categorisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The IRA is a proscribed organisation in the United Kingdom under the <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_Act_2000" title="Terrorism Act 2000">Terrorism Act 2000</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ta2000_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ta2000-328">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> and an unlawful organisation in the Republic of Ireland under the Offences Against the State Acts, where IRA volunteers are tried in the non-jury <a href="/wiki/Special_Criminal_Court" title="Special Criminal Court">Special Criminal Court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;n 29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-conway_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-conway-331">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> A similar system was introduced in Northern Ireland by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_(Emergency_Provisions)_Act_1973" title="Northern Ireland (Emergency Provisions) Act 1973">Northern Ireland (Emergency Provisions) Act 1973</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Diplock_court" title="Diplock court">Diplock court</a> consisting of a single judge and no jury.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> The IRA rejected the authority of the courts in Northern Ireland and the Republic of Ireland, and its <a href="/wiki/General_order" title="General order">standing orders</a> did not allow volunteers on trial in a criminal court to enter a <a href="/wiki/Plea" title="Plea">plea</a> or recognise the authority of the court, doing so could lead to expulsion from the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;n 30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-\xc3\x93_Faole\xc3\xa1n_333-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc3\x93_Faole\xc3\xa1n-333">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> These orders were relaxed in 1976 due to sentences in the Republic of Ireland for IRA membership being increased from two years to seven years imprisonment.<sup id="cite_ref-\xc3\x93_Faole\xc3\xa1n_333-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc3\x93_Faole\xc3\xa1n-333">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup> IRA prisoners in the UK and the Republic of Ireland were granted conditional early release as part of the Good Friday Agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> IRA members were often refused <a href="/wiki/Travel_visa" title="Travel visa">travel visas</a> to enter the United States, due to previous criminal convictions or because the <a href="/wiki/Immigration_and_Nationality_Act_of_1952" title="Immigration and Nationality Act of 1952">Immigration and Nationality Act</a> bars the entry of people who are members of an organisation which advocates the overthrow of a government by force.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;n 31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mcerlath_340-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcerlath-340">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>American TV news broadcasts tended to describe IRA members as "activists" and "guerrillas", while British TV news broadcasts commonly used the term "terrorists", particularly the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> as part of its editorial guidelines published in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> Republicans reject the label of terrorism, instead describing the IRA\'s activity as war, military activity, armed struggle or armed resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> The IRA prefer the terms <a href="/wiki/Freedom_fighter" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom fighter">freedom fighter</a>, soldier, <a href="/wiki/Activism" title="Activism">activist</a>, or volunteer for its members.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> The IRA has also been described as a "<a href="/wiki/Private_army" title="Private army">private army</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> The IRA saw the Irish War of Independence as a <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Guerrilla war">guerrilla war</a> which accomplished some of its aims, with some remaining "unfinished business".<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An internal British Army document written by General Sir <a href="/wiki/Mike_Jackson_(British_Army_officer)" title="Mike Jackson (British Army officer)">Mike Jackson</a> and two other senior officers was released in 2007 under the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_2000" title="Freedom of Information Act 2000">Freedom of Information Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-quillgan_274-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quillgan-274">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> It examined the British Army\'s 37 years of deployment in Northern Ireland, and described the IRA as "a professional, dedicated, highly skilled and resilient force", while loyalist paramilitaries and other republican groups were described as "little more than a collection of gangsters".<sup id="cite_ref-quillgan_274-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quillgan-274">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strength_and_support">Strength and support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Strength and support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_strength">Numerical strength</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Numerical strength">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>It is unclear how many people joined the IRA during the Troubles, as it did not keep detailed records of personnel.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneyxviii_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyxviii-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Journalists Eamonn Mallie and Patrick Bishop state roughly 8,000 people passed through the ranks of the IRA in the first 20 years of its existence, many of them leaving after arrest, retirement or disillusionment.<sup id="cite_ref-mallie12_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mallie12-352">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> McGuinness, who held a variety of leadership positions,<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;n 32&#93;</a></sup> estimated a total membership of 10,000 over the course of the Troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneyxviii_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyxviii-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The British Army estimates the IRA had 500 volunteers in July 1971, 130 in Derry and 340 in Belfast,<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;n 33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> journalist <a href="/wiki/Ed_Moloney" title="Ed Moloney">Ed Moloney</a> states by the end of the year the IRA in Belfast had over 1,200 volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneyinternment_102-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyinternment-102">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> After the late 1970s restructure,<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> the British Army estimated the IRA had 500 full-time volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-english344_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-english344-360">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> A 1978 British Army report by Brigadier <a href="/wiki/James_Glover_(British_Army_officer)" title="James Glover (British Army officer)">James Glover</a> stated that the restructured IRA did not require the same number of volunteers as the early 1970s, and that a small number of volunteers could "maintain a disproportionate level of violence".<sup id="cite_ref-taylorlw_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorlw-152">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> Journalist <a href="/wiki/Brendan_O%27Brien_(journalist)" title="Brendan O&#39;Brien (journalist)">Brendan O\'Brien</a> states by the late 1980s the IRA had roughly 300 active volunteers and 450 more in support roles,<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> while historian Richard English states in 1988 the IRA was believed to have no more than thirty experienced gunmen and bombers, with a further twenty volunteers with less experience and 500 more in support roles.<sup id="cite_ref-english344_360-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-english344-360">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> Moloney estimates in October 1996 the IRA had between 600 and 700 active volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-gacsecurity_290-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gacsecurity-290">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_from_other_countries_and_organisations">Support from other countries and organisations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Support from other countries and organisations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_arms_importation" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional IRA arms importation">Provisional IRA arms importation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AKM_automatkarbin_-_7,62x39mm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/AKM_automatkarbin_-_7%2C62x39mm.jpg/220px-AKM_automatkarbin_-_7%2C62x39mm.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="70" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/AKM_automatkarbin_-_7%2C62x39mm.jpg/330px-AKM_automatkarbin_-_7%2C62x39mm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/AKM_automatkarbin_-_7%2C62x39mm.jpg/440px-AKM_automatkarbin_-_7%2C62x39mm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2050" data-file-height="650" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AKM_automatkarbin_-_7,62x39mm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1,200 <a href="/wiki/AKM" title="AKM">AKM</a> assault rifles were donated by <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> in the 1980s<sup id="cite_ref-libya2_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libya2-363">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Semtex_H_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Semtex_H_1.jpg/220px-Semtex_H_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Semtex_H_1.jpg/330px-Semtex_H_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/Semtex_H_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="426" data-file-height="311" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Semtex_H_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Over two tonnes of the plastic explosive Semtex were donated by <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> in the 1980s<sup id="cite_ref-libya2_363-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libya2-363">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Libyan leader  Colonel <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a>, was a supplier of arms to the IRA, donating two shipments of arms in the early 1970s,<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> and another five in the mid-1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-libya_365-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libya-365">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> The final shipment in 1987 was intercepted by French authorities,<sup id="cite_ref-libya_365-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libya-365">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> but the prior four shipments included 1,200 AKM <a href="/wiki/Assault_rifle" title="Assault rifle">assault rifles</a>, 26 DShK <a href="/wiki/Heavy_machine_gun" title="Heavy machine gun">heavy machine guns</a>, 40 <a href="/wiki/General-purpose_machine_gun" title="General-purpose machine gun">general-purpose machine guns</a>, 33 <a href="/wiki/RPG-7" title="RPG-7">RPG-7</a> rocket launchers, 10 SAM-7 surface-to-air missiles, 10 LPO-50 flamethrowers, and over two tonnes of plastic explosive Semtex.<sup id="cite_ref-libya2_363-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libya2-363">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another main source of support was from <a href="/wiki/Irish_Americans" title="Irish Americans">Irish Americans</a>, who donated weapons and money.<sup id="cite_ref-cooganlinks_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooganlinks-366">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> The backbone of IRA support in the United States was the Irish Northern Aid Committee, better known as <a href="/wiki/NORAID" title="NORAID">NORAID</a>, who in addition to raising money for the families of IRA prisoners also secretly funneled money and weapons to the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> In the United States in November 1982, five men, including <a href="/wiki/Michael_Flannery" title="Michael Flannery">Michael Flannery</a> of NORAID and <a href="/wiki/George_Harrison_(Irish_republican)" title="George Harrison (Irish republican)">George Harrison</a>, were acquitted of smuggling arms to the IRA after they claimed the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA) had approved the shipment through arms dealer <a href="/wiki/George_de_Meo" title="George de Meo">George de Meo</a>, although de Meo denied any connection with the CIA.<sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup> Harrison\'s conservative estimate was that he smuggled 2,000\xe2\x80\x932,500 weapons and approximately 1,000,000 rounds of ammunition to Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> American support was weakened by the <a href="/wiki/11_September_2001_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="11 September 2001 attacks">11 September 2001 attacks</a> and the subsequent "<a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" class="mw-redirect" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The IRA had links with the <a href="/wiki/Basque_separatism" class="mw-redirect" title="Basque separatism">Basque separatist</a> group <a href="/wiki/ETA_(separatist_group)" title="ETA (separatist group)">ETA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cooganlinks_366-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooganlinks-366">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maria_McGuire" class="mw-redirect" title="Maria McGuire">Maria McGuire</a> states the IRA received fifty <a href="/wiki/Revolver" title="Revolver">revolvers</a> from ETA in exchange for explosives training.<sup id="cite_ref-geraghty_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geraghty-372">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-373" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-373">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> In 1973 the IRA was accused by the <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Spain" title="Law enforcement in Spain">Spanish police</a> of providing explosives for the assassination of Spanish prime minister <a href="/wiki/Luis_Carrero_Blanco" title="Luis Carrero Blanco">Luis Carrero Blanco</a> in Madrid, and the following year an ETA spokesman told German magazine <i>Der Spiegel</i> they had "very good relations" with the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-cooganlinks_366-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooganlinks-366">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-geraghty_372-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geraghty-372">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> In 1977 a representative of the Basque political party <a href="/wiki/Euskal_Iraultzarako_Alderdia" title="Euskal Iraultzarako Alderdia">Euskal Iraultzarako Alderdia</a> attended Sinn F\xc3\xa9in\'s 1977 ard fheis, and \xc3\x93 Br\xc3\xa1daigh had a close relationship with Basque separatists, regularly visiting the <a href="/wiki/Basque_Country_(greater_region)" title="Basque Country (greater region)">Basque region</a> between 1977 and 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-374" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-374">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup> The IRA received support from the <a href="/wiki/Palestine_Liberation_Organization" title="Palestine Liberation Organization">Palestine Liberation Organization</a> (PLO) in the 1970s, with volunteers attending training camps in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> In 1977 a shipment of arms from the PLO was seized in <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a>, Belgium.<sup id="cite_ref-boyneplo_376-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyneplo-376">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup> The shipment included twenty-nine <a href="/wiki/AK-47" title="AK-47">AK-47</a> assault rifles, twenty-nine French <a href="/wiki/Submachine_gun" title="Submachine gun">submachine guns</a>, seven RPG-7 rocket launchers and sixty rocket-propelled grenades, two <a href="/wiki/Bren_light_machine_gun" title="Bren light machine gun">Bren light machine guns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(weapon)" title="Mortar (weapon)">mortars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grenade" title="Grenade">grenades</a> and ammunition.<sup id="cite_ref-boyneplo_376-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyneplo-376">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup> PLO leader <a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">Yasser Arafat</a> distanced himself from the IRA following the assassination of Lord Mountbatten in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 1996, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Security_Service" title="Federal Security Service">Federal Security Service</a>, Russia\'s internal security service, accused Estonia of arms smuggling, and claimed that the IRA had bought weapons from arms dealers linked to Estonia\'s volunteer defence force, <a href="/wiki/Kaitseliit" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaitseliit">Kaitseliit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-378" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-378">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> In 2001, three Irishmen, known as the <a href="/wiki/Colombia_Three" title="Colombia Three">Colombia Three</a>, were arrested and accused of training Colombian guerrillas, the <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Armed_Forces_of_Colombia" title="Revolutionary Armed Forces of Colombia">Revolutionary Armed Forces of Colombia</a> (FARC).<sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moloneyfarc_380-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyfarc-380">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> The Irish Minister for Justice, Equality and Law Reform stated the IRA was to be paid up to $35&#160;million to train FARC in bomb-making techniques, including <a href="/wiki/Shaped_charge" title="Shaped charge">shaped charges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane_bomb" title="Propane bomb">propane bombs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landmine" class="mw-redirect" title="Landmine">landmines</a> and the construction of mortars.<sup id="cite_ref-moloneyfarc_380-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloneyfarc-380">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimerfarc_381-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimerfarc-381">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> In 2005 a commander in the <a href="/wiki/National_Army_of_Colombia" title="National Army of Colombia">National Army of Colombia</a> stated IRA techniques were being used all over Colombia by FARC, and British military experts confirmed bombs used by FARC had previously been used by the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimerfarc_381-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimerfarc-381">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> The Colombia Three were acquitted at trial in April 2004, before this was reversed at an appeal court in December 2004 although the men had fled the country and returned to Ireland before the appeal court verdict.<sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimerfarc_381-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimerfarc-381">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_activities">Other activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sectarian_attacks">Sectarian attacks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sectarian attacks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The IRA publicly condemned sectarianism and sectarian attacks, however some IRA members did carry out sectarian attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-english173_382-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-english173-382">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> Of those killed by the IRA, Malcolm Sutton classifies 130 (about 7%) of them as sectarian killings of Protestants, 88 of them committed between 1974 and 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-383" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-383">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> Unlike loyalists, the IRA denied responsibility for sectarian attacks and the members involved used cover names, such as "<a href="/wiki/South_Armagh_Republican_Action_Force" title="South Armagh Republican Action Force">Republican Action Force</a>", which was used to claim responsibility for the 1976 <a href="/wiki/Kingsmill_massacre" title="Kingsmill massacre">Kingsmill massacre</a> where ten Protestant civilians were killed in a gun attack.<sup id="cite_ref-384" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-384">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-385" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-385">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> They stated that their attacks on Protestants were retaliation for attacks on Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-english173_382-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-english173-382">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> Many in the IRA opposed these sectarian attacks, but others deemed them effective in preventing similar attacks on Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-386" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-386">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> Robert White, a professor at the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_University" title="Indiana University">Indiana University</a>, states the IRA was generally not a sectarian organisation,<sup id="cite_ref-387" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-387">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> and Rachel Kowalski from the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_War_Studies,_King%27s_College_London" title="Department of War Studies, King&#39;s College London">Department of War Studies</a>, King\'s College London states that the IRA acted in a way that was mostly blind to religious diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-388" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-388">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Protestants in the rural border areas of counties <a href="/wiki/County_Fermanagh" title="County Fermanagh">Fermanagh</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Tyrone" title="County Tyrone">Tyrone</a>, where the number of members of the security forces killed was high, viewed the IRA\'s campaign as <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-leahysect_389-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leahysect-389">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_Patterson_(historian)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Henry Patterson (historian) (page does not exist)">Henry Patterson</a>, a professor at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Ulster" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Ulster">University of Ulster</a>, concludes that while the IRA\'s campaign was unavoidably sectarian, it did not amount to ethnic cleansing.<sup id="cite_ref-390" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-390">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup> Although the IRA did not specifically target these people because of their religious affiliation, more Protestants joined the security forces so many people from that community believed the attacks were sectarian.<sup id="cite_ref-leahysect_389-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leahysect-389">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> McKearney argues that due to the British government\'s Ulsterisation policy increasing the role of the locally recruited RUC and UDR, the IRA had no choice but to target them because of their local knowledge, but acknowledges that Protestants viewed this as a sectarian attack on their community.<sup id="cite_ref-leahysect_389-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leahysect-389">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financing">Financing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Financing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>To fund
